15: Quick-TRANSfer stage steps exceeded maximum (= 7948900)<< not sure what that means <<< also attached the scripts we are using , and than a folder called enhanced that were made but never used not sure if has important ideas, ... so listeria is our FN proxy that does not have MegL capability but ahes EUT, and not sure if other infections have aspects we can look at like say produce toxin .... >>>>>> this was some info that i thouht was interesting that maybe we can use >>The Two-Stage Model is the classic framework used to explain how preeclampsia (PE) develops. Proposed primarily by Christopher Redman in the 1990s, it breaks the disease down into a placental root cause and a maternal systemic reaction.
Here is a breakdown of the two stages:
Stage 1: Abnormal Placentation (The Placenta)
This occurs early in pregnancy (1st trimester).
The Issue: Normally, specialized fetal cells (trophoblasts) remodel the mother’s spiral arteries into wide, low-resistance channels to ensure steady blood flow to the fetus.
The Failure: In PE, this remodeling is "defective." The arteries remain narrow, leading to placental ischemia (restricted blood flow) and oxidative stress. The placenta becomes "stressed" because it isn't getting enough oxygen or nutrients.
Stage 2: Maternal Systemic Syndrome (The Mother)
This occurs later in pregnancy (after 20 weeks) and produces the clinical symptoms.
The Bridge: The stressed placenta releases "factors" (like sFlt-1 and inflammatory cytokines) into the mother’s bloodstream.
The Result: These factors cause widespread endothelial dysfunction (damage to the lining of blood vessels). This leads to the classic signs of preeclampsia:
High blood pressure (hypertension).
Leaky kidneys (proteinuria).
Organ damage (liver, brain, or lungs).
Why the image mentions a "Gap"
While the Two-Stage Model is the gold standard, it has flaws that your image highlights:
The "Subset" Problem: Many women have "defective placentation" (Stage 1) but never develop the full maternal syndrome (Stage 2). The model doesn't explain what "triggers" the transition in some women but not others.
The Inflammatory Trigger: Modern research suggests there might be a "Stage 0" or an external trigger. Your image points to oral microbiome dysbiosis (specifically Fn, or Fusobacterium nucleatum).
The Microbiome Link: The theory is that bacteria from the mouth (due to gum disease/periodontitis) can travel through the blood to the placenta. This infection might be the "extra push" that causes a struggling placenta to release the toxic factors that trigger Stage 2.
In short: The Two-Stage Model explains how the body fails, but it doesn't fully explain why it happens to some people and not others—which is where the study of the microbiome and inflammation comes in.
Would you like to know more about how oral health specifically impacts placental inflammation?




what is sFlt-1 , whchi cells produce normally which in preclampsia
sFlt-1 (soluble fms-like tyrosine kinase-1) is a protein that plays a critical role in the development of preeclampsia. It is a "soluble" version of a receptor that normally sits on the surface of blood vessels.
What is sFlt-1?
Think of sFlt-1 as a "molecular sponge" or decoy receptor.
The Normal Process: To stay healthy, blood vessels need "growth factors" called VEGF (Vascular Endothelial Growth Factor) and PlGF (Placental Growth Factor). These factors act like "food" for the lining of the blood vessels (the endothelium), keeping them flexible and open.
The Problem in Preeclampsia: sFlt-1 floats freely in the mother’s blood and binds to these growth factors before they can reach the blood vessels. By "soaking up" the growth factors, sFlt-1 leaves the mother’s blood vessels "starved," leading to the inflammation, high blood pressure, and kidney damage seen in preeclampsia.
Which cells produce sFlt-1?
The production of sFlt-1 changes significantly between a healthy state and preeclampsia:
State	Primary Cell Sources	Key Characteristics
Normal (Healthy)	Endothelial cells (lining blood vessels), Monocytes (immune cells), and the Placenta.	Produced in small, regulated amounts to manage blood vessel growth. Levels rise slightly toward the end of a normal pregnancy.
Preeclampsia	Placental Trophoblasts (specifically syncytiotrophoblasts and cytotrophoblasts).	The placenta becomes the "factory" for sFlt-1. Due to low oxygen (hypoxia), these cells overproduce sFlt-1 at levels 20–50 times higher than normal.
The "Placental-Specific" Variant
Recent research has identified a specific version of this protein called sFlt-1 e15a.
In healthy people, the body mostly makes a version called sFlt-1 i13.
In women with preeclampsia, the placenta pumps out massive amounts of the e15a variant. This variant is unique to humans and higher primates, which may explain why preeclampsia is a disease that only affects humans.
Why this matters clinically
Doctors now use the sFlt-1/PlGF ratio as a diagnostic tool.
High Ratio: Indicates too much "sponge" (sFlt-1) and too little "food" (PlGF), suggesting a high risk that preeclampsia will develop or worsen within the next 1–4 weeks.
Low Ratio: Highly reliable for "ruling out" the disease for a short period, helping avoid unnecessary hospitalizations.
Would you like to know the specific ratio numbers doctors look for to predict a delivery within the next week? <<<<<<<<< can you do a deep anlysis of teh project the goals what e are aiming for and help enhance the anlysis give the scripts to do so also if ther is accesable public data we culd integrate intot heanlyis identify site give script to d that and explin reasoning

Yes — and the run failure now makes sense.

The gc() block was not an error; it was just R printing memory usage after garbage collection. The actual stop was this line:

Error in qs::qsave(...) : basic_ios::clear: iostream error

Given your folder contents, the most likely cause is disk exhaustion during serialization, not a biology problem. qs::qsave() supports higher-compression presets such as "archive", but each saved Seurat object is still huge, and your sweep was writing many 6–6.6 GB files. The console also shows peak memory got up to about 128 GB, so the sweep is stressing both disk and RAM.

Your current outputs are still useful. The uploaded Harmony plots look reasonable: cell types remain cleanly separated, donor mixing looks good, and infection is not dominating the manifold globally. That makes HVG=4000, PC=40 a strong interim candidate, even before finishing 5000. Also, your base biology is coherent: the Cell Systems explant paper used early placental explants from 6–14 post-conception weeks, profiled 158,978 cells, and showed broad inflammatory responses to P. falciparum, L. monocytogenes, and T. gondii across placental lineages.

What is already good

Your current pipeline has several strong pieces:

modular scripts with logs and legends

a reproducible Seurat workflow

parameter sensitivity started rather than assumed

a plausible integrated embedding

1-s2.0-S2405471224001170-main

 

1-s2.0-S2405471224001170-main

What is not yet publication-worthy

The main statistical weakness is that the current inference is still too close to cell-level exploration and small-sample summaries. For publication-level claims, especially with repeated donors and infection/time structure, you should make the inferential unit the donor/sample, not the cell. Pseudobulk methods and donor-aware models are strongly preferred because cell-level tests otherwise underestimate variance and inflate false discoveries.

The second big limitation is FLT1 isoform biology. In your current Seurat object, FLT1 is a gene-level signal. That is useful, but it does not separate membrane FLT1 from soluble isoforms, and it definitely does not cleanly isolate the placental-specific sFLT1-e15a variant unless you have exon/junction-aware quantification or a targeted assay. The e15a isoform is biologically important because it is placental, bioactive, and elevated in preeclampsia.

So the right framing is:

scRNA: test total FLT1, PGF, hypoxia/angiogenic stress programs, and cell-type source

bulk / targeted assay: test e15a specifically

My read of the project goal

The most publication-worthy version of this study is not “Fn causes inflammation in placenta.”

It is:

A mechanistic placenta-niche model linking stromal ethanolamine handling, trophoblast susceptibility, and an endothelial/antiangiogenic output axis that explains why some pathogens or states behave like preeclampsia triggers and others do not.

That lets you connect:

Entry niche
trophoblast attachment / susceptible state

Nutritional niche
FIB2-like stromal reservoir and EA liberation/recycling

Toxic output
FLT1/PGF imbalance, IL1B, oxidative stress, endothelial injury programs

Specificity
why EA-using organisms like Listeria may still differ clinically from Fn if the downstream toxic module is absent or different

That is also where your uploaded spatial atlas becomes very important: the Nature Medicine placenta atlas identifies FIB2 as a signaling hub with AREG/HGF → EGFR/MET toward trophoblast and VEGFA → KDR toward endothelium, which is exactly the kind of stromal–trophoblast–vascular bridge your model needs. 

Spatial multiomic landscape of …

What I would add now
1. Ethanolamine proxy analysis

This should become a central result.

Use two module families:

EA liberation / availability

PLD1, PLD2

GDPD1, GDPD5

FAAH, NAPEPLD

EA recycling / sink

ETNK1, ETNK2

PCYT2

SELENOI (EPT1)

CEPT1

Then define:

EA_release_score

EA_recycle_score

EA_proxy = EA_release_score - EA_recycle_score

Run this:

by cell type

by infection

by time

with UI matched controls

using donor-aware pseudobulk / mixed models

This directly tests your “metabolic reservoir” idea.

2. FLT1 / PGF / vascular stress axis

Use:

FLT1

PGF

KDR

VEGFA

ENG

HIF1A

EPAS1

endothelial dysfunction / inflammatory markers such as IL1B, CXCL8, PTGS2, ICAM1, VCAM1, HMOX1

Core outputs:

per-cell-type pseudobulk contrasts

FLT1, PGF, and FLT1/PGF proxy plots by infection/time

trophoblast-only and endothelial-only analyses

UI as matched baseline

The uploaded Cell Systems dataset already showed a pathogen can perturb this axis in trophoblasts, so this is a very defensible direction. 

1-s2.0-S2405471224001170-main

3. Pathogen specificity matrix

Do not overclaim that every organism with EA metabolism has the same preeclampsia mechanism.

A much stronger paper figure is a comparative matrix:

placental attachment/crossing

ethanolamine utilization / BMCs

H2S-generating capacity

endothelial dysfunction evidence

antiangiogenic / FLT1-linked evidence

preeclampsia association

The best comparator set is:

Fn: MegL/H2S virulence axis is real and contributes to virulence.

Listeria: clearly uses ethanolamine/BMC biology, making it a good EA-positive comparator, but it is not a classic PE vascular syndrome organism.

Porphyromonas gingivalis: strong oral/endothelial comparator because it directly drives endothelial dysfunction, though not via the same EA–MegL mechanism.

That figure will help reviewers see that you are testing specificity, not just telling a story.

4. Spatial anchoring

Map your EA and FLT1 modules into a spatial reference.

You already have:

the 2024 Nature Medicine first-trimester spatial multiomic placenta atlas

the 2023 spatial decidual remodeling timeline

the Cell Systems infection atlas

Use them to ask:

where are FIB2-like stromal cells relative to trophoblast and endothelium?

does the putative EA niche sit next to EVT/vCTB/endothelial programs?

are your highest EA_proxy cells spatially plausible as a nutrient reservoir?

The spatial atlas explicitly supports stromal–vascular and stromal–trophoblast crosstalk through FIB2. 

Spatial multiomic landscape of …

5. Cell–cell communication

This is a must-add for publication quality.

Use CellChat / NicheNet / CellPhoneDB-style analyses to test:

FIB2 → trophoblast: AREG/HGF → EGFR/MET

FIB2 → endothelium: VEGFA → KDR

HBC / PAMM1 → trophoblast/core: IL1B → IL1R1, chemokines, LGALS3-related axes

The original Cell Systems paper already used CellPhoneDBv4 to look at infection-induced interactions, so extending that logic to your mechanistic axes is very natural. 

1-s2.0-S2405471224001170-main

6. Public validation

This is where the story gets much stronger.

Best immediate public additions:

GSE75010

large placental PE bulk dataset, 157 placental samples

ideal for validating:

EA proxy

FLT1

PGF

hypoxia/angiogenic signatures

PE vs control and, if metadata allow, subtype comparisons.

GSE173193

public placental single-cell dataset used in recent PE papers

useful to validate:

fibroblast/PMSC senescence

trophoblast FLT1 axis

whether your EA/FLT1 modules reproduce in PE.

Bhalla 2023 spatial PE dataset

public spatial placental dataset with PE-associated RNA patterns

useful for spatial validation of your FLT1 / angiogenic / stromal modules.

Also, the Cell Systems infection dataset itself is publicly available in ArrayExpress E-MTAB-12795 and browsable through the reproductive cell atlas, so your current dataset is already externally traceable. 

1-s2.0-S2405471224001170-main

What to change in the current sweep script

Do this first:

# do NOT save every combo as a full Seurat object
save_combo_objects <- FALSE

Then only save:

the base object

HVG-level PCA objects

2–3 finalist combo objects

and save finalists as lite objects for plotting

Use:

save_combo_lite <- function(obj, path) {
  keep_red <- intersect(c("pca", "umap_rna", "harmony", "umap_harmony"), names(obj@reductions))
  lite <- DietSeurat(
    object = obj,
    assays = "RNA",
    layers = "data",
    dimreducs = keep_red,
    graphs = NULL,
    misc = TRUE
  )
  qs::qsave(lite, path, preset = "archive")
}

qs documents "archive" as the highest-compression preset, which is slower but appropriate for disk-limited archival saves.

And because your peak memory touched the machine limit, I would also run one HVG setting per fresh R session instead of the whole sweep in one session.

Script 1: donor-aware EA / FLT1 analysis

Save as scripts/10_ea_flt1_pseudobulk.R

source("R/config.R")
source("R/helpers_io.R")

suppressPackageStartupMessages({
  library(Seurat)
  library(dplyr)
  library(tidyr)
  library(ggplot2)
  library(qs)
  library(edgeR)
})

log_file <- file.path(CFG$dirs$logs, "pipeline.log")
log_msg("10_ea_flt1_pseudobulk starting.", log_file = log_file)

seu <- qs::qread(file.path(CFG$dirs$objects, "seu_with_scores.qs"))
DefaultAssay(seu) <- "RNA"

# --------------------------------------------------
# WHY:
# We already have cell types annotated. Do not redo annotation.
# We aggregate to pseudobulk because donor/sample is the inferential unit.
# --------------------------------------------------

# sample_id
if (!"sample_id" %in% colnames(seu@meta.data)) {
  seu$sample_id <- paste(
    seu[[CFG$cols$donor_id]][,1],
    seu[[CFG$cols$infection]][,1],
    seu[[CFG$cols$hpi]][,1],
    seu[[CFG$cols$stage]][,1],
    sep = "|"
  )
}

# Combined condition label
if (!"condition" %in% colnames(seu@meta.data)) {
  seu$condition <- paste0(seu[[CFG$cols$infection]][,1], "_", seu[[CFG$cols$hpi]][,1])
}

# A unique pseudobulk ID: sample + cell type
seu$pb_id <- paste(seu$sample_id, seu[[CFG$cols$cell_type]][,1], sep = "||")

# --------------------------------------------------
# Curated gene sets
# NOTE:
# FLT1 here is TOTAL FLT1 transcript signal, not sFlt1-e15a specifically.
# Isoform-specific analysis needs exon/junction-aware data or targeted assays.
# --------------------------------------------------

ea_release_genes <- c("PLD1", "PLD2", "GDPD1", "GDPD5", "FAAH", "NAPEPLD")
ea_recycle_genes <- c("ETNK1", "ETNK2", "PCYT2", "SELENOI", "CEPT1")

flt1_axis_genes  <- c("FLT1", "PGF", "KDR", "VEGFA", "ENG", "HIF1A", "EPAS1")
inflam_genes     <- c("IL1B", "CCL20", "CCL4", "CXCL8", "PTGS2", "LGALS3", "AREG", "HMOX1")

present <- function(x) intersect(x, rownames(seu))
ea_release_genes <- present(ea_release_genes)
ea_recycle_genes <- present(ea_recycle_genes)
flt1_axis_genes  <- present(flt1_axis_genes)
inflam_genes     <- present(inflam_genes)

# --------------------------------------------------
# Pseudobulk counts
# --------------------------------------------------
pb_counts <- AggregateExpression(
  object = seu,
  assays = "RNA",
  group.by = "pb_id",
  slot = "counts",
  return.seurat = FALSE
)$RNA

pb_meta <- seu@meta.data %>%
  distinct(pb_id,
           sample_id,
           donor_id = .data[[CFG$cols$donor_id]],
           infection = .data[[CFG$cols$infection]],
           hpi = .data[[CFG$cols$hpi]],
           stage = .data[[CFG$cols$stage]],
           condition,
           cell_type = .data[[CFG$cols$cell_type]]) %>%
  filter(pb_id %in% colnames(pb_counts))

# Keep only pseudobulks with enough cells
pb_ncells <- seu@meta.data %>%
  count(pb_id, name = "n_cells_pb")

pb_meta <- pb_meta %>%
  left_join(pb_ncells, by = "pb_id") %>%
  filter(n_cells_pb >= 30)

pb_counts <- pb_counts[, pb_meta$pb_id, drop = FALSE]

# --------------------------------------------------
# Normalize pseudobulk
# --------------------------------------------------
dge <- DGEList(counts = pb_counts)
dge <- calcNormFactors(dge)
logcpm <- cpm(dge, log = TRUE, prior.count = 2)

score_mean <- function(mat, genes) {
  genes <- intersect(genes, rownames(mat))
  if (length(genes) == 0) return(rep(NA_real_, ncol(mat)))
  colMeans(mat[genes, , drop = FALSE], na.rm = TRUE)
}

pb_scores <- tibble(
  pb_id = colnames(logcpm),
  EA_release = score_mean(logcpm, ea_release_genes),
  EA_recycle = score_mean(logcpm, ea_recycle_genes),
  FLT1_axis  = score_mean(logcpm, flt1_axis_genes),
  Inflamm    = score_mean(logcpm, inflam_genes),
  FLT1_expr  = if ("FLT1" %in% rownames(logcpm)) logcpm["FLT1", ] else NA_real_,
  PGF_expr   = if ("PGF"  %in% rownames(logcpm)) logcpm["PGF", ]  else NA_real_,
  IL1B_expr  = if ("IL1B" %in% rownames(logcpm)) logcpm["IL1B", ] else NA_real_,
  HMOX1_expr = if ("HMOX1" %in% rownames(logcpm)) logcpm["HMOX1", ] else NA_real_
) %>%
  mutate(
    EA_proxy = EA_release - EA_recycle,
    FLT1_PGF_diff = FLT1_expr - PGF_expr
  ) %>%
  left_join(pb_meta, by = "pb_id")

ensure_dir(CFG$dirs$tables)
write.csv(pb_scores, file.path(CFG$dirs$tables, "pseudobulk_module_scores.csv"), row.names = FALSE)

# --------------------------------------------------
# Donor-aware fixed-effect model
# WHY:
# donor is the replicate unit; infection/time are compared within donor structure
# --------------------------------------------------
fit_feature_by_celltype <- function(df, response) {
  out <- lapply(split(df, df$cell_type), function(dd) {
    # require enough samples
    if (nrow(dd) < 6 || length(unique(dd$infection)) < 2) return(NULL)

    dd$donor_id  <- factor(dd$donor_id)
    dd$infection <- factor(dd$infection)
    dd$hpi       <- factor(dd$hpi)

    formula_txt <- paste(response, "~ donor_id + infection * hpi")
    fit <- lm(as.formula(formula_txt), data = dd)
    aov_tab <- anova(fit)

    data.frame(
      cell_type = unique(dd$cell_type),
      term = rownames(aov_tab),
      df = aov_tab$Df,
      F_value = aov_tab$`F value`,
      p_value = aov_tab$`Pr(>F)`
    )
  })

  bind_rows(out)
}

ea_stats    <- fit_feature_by_celltype(pb_scores, "EA_proxy")
flt1_stats  <- fit_feature_by_celltype(pb_scores, "FLT1_PGF_diff")
inflam_stats <- fit_feature_by_celltype(pb_scores, "Inflamm")

write.csv(ea_stats,   file.path(CFG$dirs$tables, "EA_proxy_stats_by_celltype.csv"), row.names = FALSE)
write.csv(flt1_stats, file.path(CFG$dirs$tables, "FLT1_PGF_stats_by_celltype.csv"), row.names = FALSE)
write.csv(inflam_stats, file.path(CFG$dirs$tables, "Inflamm_stats_by_celltype.csv"), row.names = FALSE)

# --------------------------------------------------
# Figures
# --------------------------------------------------
plot_heat <- function(df, value_col, title_txt, outname) {
  agg <- df %>%
    group_by(cell_type, condition) %>%
    summarise(value = mean(.data[[value_col]], na.rm = TRUE), .groups = "drop")

  p <- ggplot(agg, aes(x = condition, y = cell_type, fill = value)) +
    geom_tile() +
    theme_bw() +
    labs(title = title_txt, x = NULL, y = NULL)

  save_plot(file.path(CFG$dirs$figures, outname), p, w = 10, h = 7)
}

plot_spaghetti <- function(df, value_col, title_txt, outname, keep_celltypes = NULL) {
  dd <- df
  if (!is.null(keep_celltypes)) dd <- dd %>% filter(cell_type %in% keep_celltypes)

  p <- ggplot(dd, aes(x = condition, y = .data[[value_col]], group = donor_id, color = donor_id)) +
    geom_line(alpha = 0.6) +
    geom_point(size = 2) +
    facet_wrap(~cell_type, scales = "free_y") +
    theme_bw() +
    labs(title = title_txt, x = NULL, y = value_col)

  save_plot(file.path(CFG$dirs$figures, outname), p, w = 14, h = 9)
}

plot_heat(pb_scores, "EA_proxy", "EA proxy by cell type and condition", "Fig21_EA_proxy_heatmap.pdf")
plot_heat(pb_scores, "FLT1_PGF_diff", "FLT1-PGF pseudobulk difference by cell type and condition", "Fig22_FLT1_PGF_heatmap.pdf")
plot_spaghetti(pb_scores, "EA_proxy", "EA proxy by donor across conditions", "Fig23_EA_proxy_spaghetti.pdf",
               keep_celltypes = c("F", "F_p", "F_sm", "PV", "VCT", "VCT_fusing", "Endo_f"))
plot_spaghetti(pb_scores, "FLT1_PGF_diff", "FLT1-PGF by donor across conditions", "Fig24_FLT1_PGF_spaghetti.pdf",
               keep_celltypes = c("VCT", "VCT_fusing", "Endo_f", "PV"))

# UMAP overlays on existing reductions
score_to_cells <- pb_scores %>%
  select(pb_id, EA_proxy, FLT1_expr, PGF_expr, IL1B_expr, HMOX1_expr)

cell_md <- seu@meta.data %>%
  select(pb_id) %>%
  left_join(score_to_cells, by = "pb_id")

seu$EA_proxy_pb  <- cell_md$EA_proxy
seu$FLT1_pb      <- cell_md$FLT1_expr
seu$PGF_pb       <- cell_md$PGF_expr
seu$IL1B_pb      <- cell_md$IL1B_expr
seu$HMOX1_pb     <- cell_md$HMOX1_expr

red_use <- if ("umap_harmony" %in% names(seu@reductions)) "umap_harmony" else "X_umap"

for (feat in c("EA_proxy_pb", "FLT1_pb", "PGF_pb", "IL1B_pb", "HMOX1_pb")) {
  p <- FeaturePlot(seu, features = feat, reduction = red_use, split.by = "condition", raster = TRUE)
  save_plot(file.path(CFG$dirs$figures, paste0("Fig_", feat, "_split_condition.pdf")), p, w = 16, h = 10)
}

qs::qsave(pb_scores, file.path(CFG$dirs$objects, "pseudobulk_module_scores.qs"))
log_msg("10_ea_flt1_pseudobulk done.", log_file = log_file)
Script 2: public validation in GSE75010

Save as scripts/11_validate_GSE75010.R

suppressPackageStartupMessages({
  library(GEOquery)
  library(limma)
  library(dplyr)
  library(ggplot2)
})

outdir <- "outputs/public_validation"
dir.create(outdir, recursive = TRUE, showWarnings = FALSE)

# -----------------------------------------
# WHY:
# GSE75010 is a large public placental PE dataset.
# We use it to validate the EA proxy + FLT1 axis in independent data.
# -----------------------------------------
gse <- getGEO("GSE75010", GSEMatrix = TRUE)
eset <- gse[[1]]

expr <- exprs(eset)
pheno <- pData(eset)
feat <- fData(eset)

# inspect phenotype columns once
write.csv(pheno, file.path(outdir, "GSE75010_pheno_raw.csv"))

# try to find a usable gene symbol column
gene_col <- c("Gene Symbol", "GENE_SYMBOL", "Symbol", "gene_assignment")
gene_col <- gene_col[gene_col %in% colnames(feat)][1]
if (is.na(gene_col)) stop("Could not identify gene-symbol column in feature data.")

feat$gene_symbol <- feat[[gene_col]]
keep <- !is.na(feat$gene_symbol) & feat$gene_symbol != ""
expr <- expr[keep, , drop = FALSE]
feat <- feat[keep, , drop = FALSE]

rownames(expr) <- feat$gene_symbol
expr <- avereps(expr)

# crude phenotype parsing; adjust after inspecting GSE75010_pheno_raw.csv
pheno_txt <- apply(pheno, 1, function(x) paste(x, collapse = " | "))
group <- ifelse(grepl("preeclamp", pheno_txt, ignore.case = TRUE), "PE",
         ifelse(grepl("control|normotensive|normal", pheno_txt, ignore.case = TRUE), "Control", NA))
pheno$group_simple <- group

use <- !is.na(pheno$group_simple)
expr <- expr[, use, drop = FALSE]
pheno <- pheno[use, , drop = FALSE]

ea_release <- intersect(c("PLD1","PLD2","GDPD1","GDPD5","FAAH","NAPEPLD"), rownames(expr))
ea_recycle <- intersect(c("ETNK1","ETNK2","PCYT2","SELENOI","CEPT1"), rownames(expr))
flt1_axis  <- intersect(c("FLT1","PGF","KDR","VEGFA","ENG","HIF1A","EPAS1"), rownames(expr))

score_mean <- function(mat, genes) {
  if (length(genes) == 0) return(rep(NA_real_, ncol(mat)))
  colMeans(mat[genes, , drop = FALSE], na.rm = TRUE)
}

scores <- data.frame(
  sample = colnames(expr),
  group = pheno$group_simple,
  EA_release = score_mean(expr, ea_release),
  EA_recycle = score_mean(expr, ea_recycle),
  FLT1_axis  = score_mean(expr, flt1_axis),
  FLT1 = if ("FLT1" %in% rownames(expr)) expr["FLT1", ] else NA_real_,
  PGF  = if ("PGF"  %in% rownames(expr)) expr["PGF", ]  else NA_real_
) %>%
  mutate(
    EA_proxy = EA_release - EA_recycle,
    FLT1_PGF_diff = FLT1 - PGF
  )

write.csv(scores, file.path(outdir, "GSE75010_scores.csv"), row.names = FALSE)

design <- model.matrix(~ 0 + group, data = scores)
colnames(design) <- gsub("^group", "", colnames(design))
fit <- lmFit(expr[, scores$sample], design)
cont <- makeContrasts(PE_vs_Control = PE - Control, levels = design)
fit2 <- eBayes(contrasts.fit(fit, cont))
tt <- topTable(fit2, number = Inf, sort.by = "P")
write.csv(tt, file.path(outdir, "GSE75010_PE_vs_Control_limma.csv"))

for (feat in c("EA_proxy", "FLT1_axis", "FLT1", "PGF", "FLT1_PGF_diff")) {
  p <- ggplot(scores, aes(x = group, y = .data[[feat]], fill = group)) +
    geom_boxplot(outlier.shape = NA) +
    geom_jitter(width = 0.15) +
    theme_bw() +
    labs(title = paste("GSE75010:", feat))
  ggsave(file.path(outdir, paste0("GSE75010_", feat, ".pdf")), p, width = 6, height = 5)
}
What I added and why

I would count these additions as the core upgrades:

Donor-aware pseudobulk modeling
because that is the right inferential level for publication.

EA proxy axis
because it directly tests your central metabolic niche idea instead of treating it as narrative only.

FLT1/PGF antiangiogenic axis
because it connects your infection model to the clinical PE phenotype and is already supported by the uploaded Cell Systems paper’s P. falciparum results. 

1-s2.0-S2405471224001170-main

FIB2 spatial anchoring
because your best mechanistic paper will show not only “who expresses it” but also “where the niche is.” The spatial atlas already places FIB2 into trophoblast/endothelial signaling neighborhoods. 

Spatial multiomic landscape of …

External validation
because reviewers will trust the mechanism much more if it reproduces in public PE data.

Final judgment

You are not far off from a strong paper direction.

Right now, the project is already good at:

exploratory single-cell structure

cell-type interpretation

infection-context framing

a compelling mechanistic hypothesis

What it still needs for a publication-worthy package is:

donor-level inference

mechanism-specific figures

spatial anchoring

external validation

explicit reporting of effect sizes, uncertainty, and the correct testing level for the hierarchical design, which is exactly the standard expected in strong journals.

Ethanolamine proxy analysis
This should become a central result.
Use two module families:
EA liberation / availability
	• PLD1, PLD2
	• GDPD1, GDPD5
	• FAAH, NAPEPLD
EA recycling / sink
	• ETNK1, ETNK2
	• PCYT2
	• SELENOI (EPT1)
	• CEPT1
Then define:
	• EA_release_score
	• EA_recycle_score
	• EA_proxy = EA_release_score - EA_recycle_score
Run this:
	• by cell type
	• by infection
	• by time
	• with UI matched controls
	• using donor-aware pseudobulk / mixed models
This directly tests your “metabolic reservoir” idea.
2. FLT1 / PGF / vascular stress axis
Use:
	• FLT1
	• PGF
	• KDR
	• VEGFA
	• ENG
	• HIF1A
	• EPAS1
	• endothelial dysfunction / inflammatory markers such as IL1B, CXCL8, PTGS2, ICAM1, VCAM1, HMOX1
Core outputs:
	• per-cell-type pseudobulk contrasts
	• FLT1, PGF, and FLT1/PGF proxy plots by infection/time
	• trophoblast-only and endothelial-only analyses
	• UI as matched baseline
The uploaded Cell Systems dataset already showed a pathogen can perturb this axis in trophoblasts, so this is a very defensible direction.
3. Pathogen specificity matrix
Do not overclaim that every organism with EA metabolism has the same preeclampsia mechanism.
A much stronger paper figure is a comparative matrix:
	• placental attachment/crossing
	• ethanolamine utilization / BMCs
	• H2S-generating capacity
	• endothelial dysfunction evidence
	• antiangiogenic / FLT1-linked evidence
	• preeclampsia association
The best comparator set is:
	• Fn: MegL/H2S virulence axis is real and contributes to virulence. (PubMed)
	• Listeria: clearly uses ethanolamine/BMC biology, making it a good EA-positive comparator, but it is not a classic PE vascular syndrome organism. (PubMed)
	• Porphyromonas gingivalis: strong oral/endothelial comparator because it directly drives endothelial dysfunction, though not via the same EA–MegL mechanism. (Nature)
That figure will help reviewers see that you are testing specificity, not just telling a story.
4. Spatial anchoring
Map your EA and FLT1 modules into a spatial reference.
You already have:
	• the 2024 Nature Medicine first-trimester spatial multiomic placenta atlas
	• the 2023 spatial decidual remodeling timeline
	• the Cell Systems infection atlas
Use them to ask:
	• where are FIB2-like stromal cells relative to trophoblast and endothelium?
	• does the putative EA niche sit next to EVT/vCTB/endothelial programs?
	• are your highest EA_proxy cells spatially plausible as a nutrient reservoir?
The spatial atlas explicitly supports stromal–vascular and stromal–trophoblast crosstalk through FIB2.
5. Cell–cell communication
This is a must-add for publication quality.
Use CellChat / NicheNet / CellPhoneDB-style analyses to test:
	• FIB2 → trophoblast: AREG/HGF → EGFR/MET
	• FIB2 → endothelium: VEGFA → KDR
	• HBC / PAMM1 → trophoblast/core: IL1B → IL1R1, chemokines, LGALS3-related axes

I went through the thesis notes, both GitHub repos, the uploaded outputs, and the original Cell Systems infection paper. The core hypothesis is clear and genuinely novel: an Ethanolamine–MegL Toxic Switch in which a placental EA-rich niche supports Fusobacterium nucleatum colonization, EA depletion derepresses megL, and the resulting H₂S + NH₃ output drives a PE-like vascular syndrome. Your notes already articulate that logic, its novelty, and five testable predictions, including ΔmegL rescue, EA supplementation, GSE75010 validation, and a comparative Listeria control.

Two quick housekeeping notes first. Some earlier uploads have expired on my side, so if you want me to re-read any specific older file, re-upload it. And yes, the basic_ios::clear: iostream error from the parameter sweep is most consistent with a disk write failure during qs::qsave(), not a biological failure; qs does support higher-compression presets such as "archive", but writing many multi-GB Seurat objects will still fill a drive quickly.

Bottom line

The project is already strong enough for a good thesis, but it is not yet publication-grade in its current form. The main gap is that the current workflow is still too centered on exploratory cell-level plots and composite scores. To make it paper-ready, the story needs to shift to:

donor-aware inference

cell-type-resolved mechanistic modules

spatial anchoring

specificity controls

external validation in PE datasets

That is a very fixable problem, not a conceptual one. The good news is that your current data, the architecture repo, and the public datasets are enough to do it. The Cell Systems paper already used donor-corrected limma/edgeR for infected-vs-uninfected comparisons and CellPhoneDB v4 for ligand–receptor inference, so that standard is directly compatible with what you are trying to build.

What is already good

The infection dataset itself is excellent. The original study profiled placental explants from 5–14 post-conception weeks, retained all major placental lineages in culture, and generated a 158,978-cell infected scRNA/snRNA resource spanning P. falciparum, L. monocytogenes, and T. gondii. It showed a broad inflammatory program across lineages, strong trophoblast involvement, and pathogen-specific biology, including IL1B responses for P. falciparum and Listeria and an altered FLT1/PGF axis in trophoblasts after P. falciparum exposure. That gives you a real biological backbone, not just a hypothesis scaffold.

Your architecture repo is also thinking in the right direction. The forked hPlacenta-architecture repo explicitly frames a Vicious Cycle spatial pipeline integrating a multiome reference with Slide-tags and STARmap-ISS, and the updated README already adds exactly the kinds of publication-grade safeguards reviewers like: k-sensitivity robustness, week-stratified rewiring, density-aware spatial controls, a spatially constrained ligand–receptor null model, and pseudobulk niche DE by donor/week. Those are excellent design choices and should be ported conceptually into the infection pipeline.

Your thesis and presentation are also already sharper than most draft hypotheses. They do three valuable things: they define the EA-rich state vs EA-depleted toxic-switch state, they explain why Listeria is a useful EA-positive / MegL-negative comparator, and they identify exactly what is still novel rather than pretending it is already proven. That framing is a major strength.

What is weak right now

The biggest weakness is statistical unit choice. For publication, the inferential unit should be the donor/sample, not the cell. Current best-practice guidance is very clear that condition-level DE in scRNA-seq should use pseudobulk aggregation or an explicit random-effect framework to account for within-sample correlation, because naive cell-level testing inflates evidence.

The second weakness is claim resolution. In your current Seurat object, FLT1 is a gene-level signal. That is useful, but it is not the same thing as measuring the placental soluble isoform sFLT1-e15a. The literature supports e15a as a placental, primate-specific, and major pathogenic soluble FLT1 isoform in PE, so your scRNA analysis should be framed as a FLT1/PGF proxy, not a direct e15a readout.

The third weakness is repo organization. The infection repo’s public GitHub tree currently exposes mainly helper files in R/ (config.R, helpers_io.R, helpers_plot.R, helpers_scores.R), while the numbered working scripts appear more fully only in the uploaded bundles; that makes reproduction from GitHub alone weaker than it should be. Meanwhile, the spatial repo root mixes code, PDFs, presentations, outputs, scripts, and data-heavy artifacts in one place, which is workable for a lab notebook but not ideal for long-term reproducibility.

My critique of the two repos

For placenta-infection-pipeline, the good is modularity, helper functions, logging, saved figures, and a clear progression from object construction to sensitivity analysis. The main improvement needed is to promote it from a plotting pipeline to an inference pipeline: pseudobulk tables, effect sizes, donor-aware models, and explicit negative/positive comparator logic should be first-class outputs, not add-ons. The current public repo also needs the numbered analysis scripts committed clearly, with one README that says exactly which script produces which figure and which object.

For hPlacenta-architecture, the conceptual direction is already strong. The README is unusually good because it makes the biological goal explicit, offers a minimal and an advanced pipeline, and documents outputs and robustness additions. The optional CellChat module is also well designed: it skips gracefully when heavy dependencies are not installed and runs by week, which is exactly how a heavy optional module should behave. The main improvement needed is focus: it should be tightened around the MegL toxic-switch use case, especially around FIB2, trophoblast–vascular interfaces, and spatial evidence that can actually be transferred back into the infection analysis.

What I would add, and why it improves the thesis
1. Ethanolamine proxy analysis

This should be a central result, not a side panel. Your own notes already propose an EA availability score using GDPD1, GDPD5, PLD1, FAAH versus ETNK1, PCYT2, and the presentation extends the logic to a broader liberation/recycling program. That is exactly the right direction.

comprehensive_repo_and_analysis_review.md
# Comprehensive review of the two repositories and the current analysis process

## Repositories reviewed

### 1) RustemKurkcu/placenta-infection-pipeline
This repository is the focused single-cell infection pipeline and currently exposes helper infrastructure in the `R/` directory (`config.R`, `helpers_io.R`, `helpers_plot.R`, `helpers_scores.R`). The actively used numbered scripts were reviewed from the uploaded script bundle and outputs.

### 2) RustemKurkcu/hPlacenta-architecture
This repository is a fork of `jian-shu-lab/hPlacenta-architecture` and includes `BO'sWork`, `RPAC`, `config`, `data`, `docs`, `output`, `scripts`, plus `RUN_PIPELINE_MINIMAL.R`, `RUN_PIPELINE_ADVANCED.R`, and an optional `06C_cellchat_optional.R` module. The updated README positions it as a “Vicious Cycle” spatial pipeline integrating a multiome RNA reference with Slide-tags and STARmap-ISS, and explicitly adds k-sensitivity, spatial nulls, and pseudobulk niche DE by donor/week.

## What is strong in the infection pipeline

- Clean modular structure.
- Good use of logs, legends, and saved outputs.
- Existing cell-type annotations are reusable and should be preserved.
- Strong biological starting point because the Cell Systems explant study already demonstrated donor-aware differential analysis with limma/edgeR, CellPhoneDB-based interaction analysis, and pathogen-specific responses.

## What is weak in the infection pipeline

- Too much emphasis on cell-level or score-level exploration without enough donor-level inference.
- Early parameter-sweep implementation was not disk-safe.
- Harmony sensitivity was useful as QC, but it is not itself a biological result.
- Current proxy analyses need better calibration, stronger negative controls, and explicit limitations.

## What is strong in the spatial architecture pipeline

- The advanced README is already thinking like a reviewer: k-robustness, week-stratified rewiring, density-aware nulls, and donor/week pseudobulk are exactly the types of safeguards that prevent overclaiming.
- The optional CellChat module is carefully written to skip gracefully and to run by week.
- The project is already aimed at spatial vulnerability and niche logic, which is the right conceptual complement to the infection pipeline.

## What is weak in the spatial architecture pipeline

- It is still presented partly as a broad “vulnerability” framework rather than being tightly yoked to the MegL toxic-switch model.
- The README says some key EA genes are absent from measured spatial panels, which means the organoid or cross-platform layer becomes essential rather than optional.
- There is a risk of using many composite spatial scores without enough orthogonal validation.

## Best combined strategy

- Use the infection pipeline to establish **who changes under infection**.
- Use the architecture pipeline to establish **where the permissive niche exists**.
- Use public PE datasets to establish **whether the same host-side program is clinically relevant**.
- Use a pathogen specificity matrix to establish **why this is not just generic infection biology**.

## Most important methodological upgrades

1. Donor-aware pseudobulk DE and module analysis by cell type.
2. Reference mapping from architecture labels into infection fibroblast/perivascular states.
3. Spatial hotspot projection of EA and FLT1-related host modules.
4. Targeted rather than generic cell-cell communication analysis.
5. Public PE validation.
6. Disk-safe object management and session-level reproducibility.

## Best candidate headline for the thesis/paper

A host-defined placental ethanolamine niche and stromal–vascular signaling program explain how Fusobacterium nucleatum could convert placental colonization into a preeclampsia-like antiangiogenic syndrome.


presentation_notes_added_analyses.md
# Added analyses: slide-ready notes

## 1) EA proxy analysis
**Why add it**
- Tests the central host-side premise of the MegL Toxic Switch: whether placental cells create an ethanolamine-rich niche.

**What is measured**
- EA liberation genes: PLD1, PLD2, GDPD1, GDPD5, FAAH, NAPEPLD
- EA sink genes: ETNK1, ETNK2, PCYT2, SELENOI, CEPT1
- Composite proxy: `EA_proxy = z(EA_release) - z(EA_recycle)`

**How it improves the thesis**
- Converts the EA niche from narrative to quantifiable result.
- Enables cell-type, time, and infection-specific comparisons.
- Provides a direct bridge to GSE75010 PE validation.

**Expected result**
- Elevated EA proxy in stromal/trophoblast niches, especially FIB2-like or trophoblast-adjacent states.

**If positive**
- Supports host metabolic permissiveness for Fn.

**If negative**
- Suggests the niche is driven by fewer genes, a different compartment, or a spatial rather than transcript-level effect.

## 2) FLT1 / PGF vascular-stress axis
**Why add it**
- Connects infection biology to the canonical PE antiangiogenic syndrome.

**What is measured**
- Exact genes: FLT1, PGF, KDR, VEGFA, ENG, HIF1A, EPAS1, IL1B, CXCL8, PTGS2, ICAM1, VCAM1, HMOX1
- Composite outputs: FLT1, PGF, FLT1-PGF difference, angiogenic-stress score

**How it improves the thesis**
- Tests whether infected trophoblast/endothelial programs look PE-relevant.
- Separates generic inflammation from a vascular/antiangiogenic phenotype.

**Expected result**
- Strongest shifts in trophoblast and endothelial compartments.

**Key caveat**
- Standard scRNA captures total FLT1, not the placental-specific sFLT1-e15a isoform.

## 3) Pathogen specificity matrix
**Why add it**
- Reviewers will ask why Listeria or other pathogens are not equivalent to Fn.

**How it improves the thesis**
- Makes the comparator logic explicit.
- Shows that the model is testing specificity instead of fitting every pathogen to the same story.

## 4) FIB2 reference mapping
**Why add it**
- The current infection labels are broad. Your mechanism needs a more specific stromal identity if you want to claim a metabolic reservoir.

**How it improves the thesis**
- Upgrades the claim from “fibroblasts may matter” to “FIB2-like stromal cells appear to matter.”

## 5) Spatial EA / FLT1 hotspot mapping
**Why add it**
- Spatial structure can show whether permissive metabolic niches and vascular vulnerability are physically adjacent.

**How it improves the thesis**
- Links expression programs to architecture.
- Provides a bridge between the infection pipeline and the hPlacenta spatial pipeline.

## 6) Targeted CellChat
**Why add it**
- Tests whether the hypothesized stromal-trophoblast-endothelial and macrophage-trophoblast axes are actually enriched.

**How it improves the thesis**
- Moves the story from single-cell states to cell-cell mechanism.

## 7) Public PE validation
**Why add it**
- A thesis/paper is stronger when the same host-side logic appears in independent PE cohorts.

**How it improves the thesis**
- Adds clinical relevance.
- Demonstrates that your host-side programs are not unique to a single experimental system.
